      <p>
       The
       <b>
        Caribbean Community
       </b>
       (
       <b>
        CARICOM
       </b>
       ) is an organization of fifteen
       <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">
        Caribbean
       </a>
       nations and dependencies whose main objective is to promote economic integration and cooperation among its members, to ensure that the benefits of integration are equitably shared, and to coordinate foreign policy.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The organization was established in 1973. Its major activities involve coordinating economic policies and development planning; devising and instituting
       <a href="/wiki/Projects_of_the_Caribbean_Community" title="Projects of the Caribbean Community">
        special projects
       </a>
       for the less-developed countries within its jurisdiction; operating as a regional single market for many of its members (Caricom Single Market); and handling regional trade disputes. The secretariat headquarters is in
       <a href="/wiki/Georgetown,_Guyana" title="Georgetown, Guyana">
        Georgetown
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Guyana" title="Guyana">
        Guyana
       </a>
       . CARICOM is an official United Nations Observer.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Established mainly by the English-speaking parts of the Caribbean, CARICOM has become multilingual in practice with the addition of
       <a href="/wiki/Dutch_language" title="Dutch language">
        Dutch-speaking
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Suriname" title="Suriname">
        Suriname
       </a>
       on 4 July 1995 and
       <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">
        French
       </a>
       - and
       <a href="/wiki/Haitian_Creole" title="Haitian Creole">
        Haitian Kreyòl
       </a>
       - speaking
       <a href="/wiki/Haiti" title="Haiti">
        Haiti
       </a>
       on 2 July 2002. Furthermore, it was suggested that
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">
        Spanish
       </a>
       should also become a working language.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In July 2012, CARICOM announced that they were considering making French and Dutch official languages.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 2001, the heads of government signed a revised
       <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Chaguaramas" title="Treaty of Chaguaramas">
        Treaty of Chaguaramas
       </a>
       that cleared the way to transform the idea of a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Common_market" title="Common market">
        common market
       </a>
       CARICOM into a
       <a href="/wiki/CARICOM_Single_Market_and_Economy" title="CARICOM Single Market and Economy">
        Caribbean (CARICOM) Single Market and Economy
       </a>
       . Part of the revised treaty establishes and implements the
       <a href="/wiki/Caribbean_Court_of_Justice" title="Caribbean Court of Justice">
        Caribbean Court of Justice
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Membership">
        Membership
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Caribbean_Community&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Membership">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Member_states_of_the_Caribbean_Community" title="Member states of the Caribbean Community">
        Member states of the Caribbean Community
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Currently CARICOM has 15 full members, 5 associate members and 8 observers. All of the associate members are
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_overseas_territories" title="British overseas territories">
        British overseas territories
       </a>
       , and it is currently not established what the role of the associate members will be. The observers are states which engage in at least one of CARICOM's technical committees.
      </p>

         Status

        </th>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Organizational_structure">
        Organizational structure
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Caribbean_Community&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Organizational structure">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Structures comprised by the overall
       <b>
        Caribbean Community
       </b>
       (
       <b>
        CARICOM
       </b>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Under Article 4 CARICOM breaks its 15 member states into two groups: Less Developed Countries (LDCs) and More Developed Countries (MDCs).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-revised_chaguaramas_10-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-revised_chaguaramas-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The countries of CARICOM which are designated as Less Developed Countries (LDCs) are:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-revised_chaguaramas_10-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-revised_chaguaramas-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Antigua &amp; Barbuda
       </li>
       <li>
        Belize
       </li>
       <li>
        Commonwealth of Dominica
       </li>
       <li>
        Grenada
       </li>
       <li>
        Republic of Haiti
       </li>
       <li>
        Montserrat
       </li>
       <li>
        Federation of St. Kitts &amp; Nevis
       </li>
       <li>
        St. Lucia
       </li>
       <li>
        St. Vincent &amp; the Grenadines
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       The countries of CARICOM which are designated as More Developed Countries (MDCs) are:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-revised_chaguaramas_10-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-revised_chaguaramas-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Commonwealth of the Bahamas
       </li>
       <li>
        Barbados
       </li>
       <li>
        Co-operative Republic of Guyana
       </li>
       <li>
        Jamaica
       </li>
       <li>
        Republic of Suriname
       </li>
       <li>
        Republic of Trinidad &amp; Tobago
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Chairmanship">
        Chairmanship
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Caribbean_Community&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Chairmanship">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The post of Chairman (Head of CARICOM) is held in rotation by the regional Heads of State (for the republics) and Heads of Government (for the realms) of CARICOM's 15 member states.These include: Antigua and Barbuda, Belize, Dominica, Grenada, Haiti, Montserrat, St. Kitts and Nevis, St. Lucia, St. Vincent and the Grenadines, Bahamas, Barbados, Guyana, Jamaica, Suriname, Trinidad and Tobago.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Heads_of_government">
        Heads of government
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Caribbean_Community&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Heads of government">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       CARICOM contains a quasi-Cabinet of the individual
       <a href="/wiki/CARICOM_Heads_of_Government" title="CARICOM Heads of Government">
        Heads of Government
       </a>
       . These heads are given specific specialised portfolios of responsibility for overall regional development and integration.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Secretariat">
        Secretariat
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Caribbean_Community&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Secretariat">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Secretariat_of_the_Caribbean_Community" title="Secretariat of the Caribbean Community">
         Secretariat of the Caribbean Community
        </a>
        , The term of office of the Secretary-General is five years, which may be renewed. (Chief Administrative Organ)
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Secretary-General_of_the_Caribbean_Community" title="Secretary-General of the Caribbean Community">
         Secretary-General of the Caribbean Community
        </a>
        , the CARICOM Secretary General (Chief Executive) handles foreign and community relations.
       </li>
       <li>
        Deputy Secretary-General of the Caribbean Community, handles human and social Development.
       </li>
       <li>
        General Counsel of the Caribbean Community, handles trade and economic integration.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       The goal statement of the CARICOM Secretariat is:
      </p>
      <blockquote class="templatequote">
       <p>
        To provide dynamic leadership and service, in partnership with Community institutions and Groups, toward the attainment of a viable, internationally competitive and sustainable Community, with improved quality of life for all.
       </p>
      </blockquote>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Organs_and_bodies">
        Organs and bodies
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Caribbean_Community&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Organs and bodies">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <caption>
        Principal organs
       </caption>
       <tr>
        <th>
         Organ
        </th>
        <th>
         Description
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         CARICOM Heads of Government
        </td>
        <td>
         Consisting of the various heads of Government from each member state
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Standing Committee of Ministers
        </td>
        <td>
         Ministerial responsibilities for specific areas, for example the Standing Committee of Ministers responsible for Health will consist of Ministers of Health from each member state
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Community_Council">
        Community Council
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Caribbean_Community&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Community Council">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       The Community Council consists of ministers responsible for community affairs and any other Minister designated by the member states in their absolute discretion. It is one of the community's principal organs; the other is the Conference of the Heads of Government. It is supported by four other organs and three bodies.
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <caption>
        Secondary organs
       </caption>
       <tr>
        <th>
         Secondary organ
        </th>
        <th>
         Abbreviation
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Council for Finance and Planning
        </td>
        <td>
         COFAP
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Council for Foreign and Community Relations
        </td>
        <td>
         COFCOR
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Council for Human and Social Development
        </td>
        <td>
         COHSOD
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Council for Trade and Economic Development
        </td>
        <td>
         COTED
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <caption>
        Bodies
       </caption>
       <tr>
        <th>
         Body
        </th>
        <th>
         Description
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Legal Affairs Committee
        </td>
        <td>
         provides legal advice
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Budget Committee
        </td>
        <td>
         examines the draft budget and work programme of the Secretariat and submits recommendations to the Community Council.
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Committee of the Central Bank Governors
        </td>
        <td>
         provides recommendations to the COFAP on monetary and financial matters.
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>

        </td>

        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Caribbean_Regional_Information_and_Translation_Institute&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Caribbean Regional Information and Translation Institute (page does not exist)">
          Caribbean Regional Information and Translation Institute
         </a>
        </td>
        <td>
         CRITI
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       <b>
        The Caribbean Court of Justice
       </b>
       will act in its "original jurisdiction", as settlement unit for disputes on the functioning of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caribbean_Single_Market_and_Economy" title="Caribbean Single Market and Economy">
        Caribbean (CARICOM) Single Market and Economy
       </a>
       (CSME). Additionally the states of CARICOM voted to supplement original jurisdiction with "appellate jurisdiction" under this the former colonies of the United Kingdom will have effectively replaced the
       <a href="/wiki/Judicial_Committee_of_the_Privy_Council" title="Judicial Committee of the Privy Council">
        Privy Council
       </a>
       in London, United Kingdom with the CCJ.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2012)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The CCJ is based in
       <a href="/wiki/Port_of_Spain" title="Port of Spain">
        Port of Spain
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Trinidad_and_Tobago" title="Trinidad and Tobago">
        Trinidad and Tobago
       </a>
       . The majority of member states
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words" title="Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words">
          <span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too vague attribution or weasel words. (February 2012)">
           which?
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       however, continue to utilize the Privy Council as their final appellate court and three member states do not use the CCJ for either its original jurisdiction or its appellate jurisdiction because they have either not signed the Revised Treaty of Chaguaramas (the Bahamas and Haiti) or are a current British colony (Montserrat).
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Associate_institutions">

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="The_CARICOM_Standard_and_CARICOM_Song">
        The CARICOM Standard and CARICOM Song
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Caribbean_Community&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: The CARICOM Standard and CARICOM Song">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The flag of the Caribbean Community was chosen and approved in November 1983 at the Conference of Heads of Government Meeting in Port of Spain, Trinidad. The original design by the firm of WINART Studies in Georgetown, Guyana was substantially modified at the July 1983 Meeting of the Conference of Heads of Government.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CARICOM_Standard_12-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-CARICOM_Standard-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The flag was first flown on 4 July 1984 in Nassau, Bahamas at the fifth Meeting of the Conference of Heads of Government.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRW_Flags_CARICOM_13-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-CRW_Flags_CARICOM-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The flag features a blue background, but the upper part is a light blue representing sky and the lower, a darker blue representing the Caribbean Sea. The yellow circle in the centre represents the sun on which is printed in black the logo of the Caribbean Community, two interlocking Cs. The two Cs are in the form of broken links in a chain, symbolising both unity and a break with the colonial past. The narrow ring of green around the sun represents the vegetation of the region.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CARICOM_Standard_12-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-CARICOM_Standard-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       For CARICOM's 40th anniversary, a competition to compose an official song or anthem for CARICOM was launched in April 2013
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-History_created_with_new_CARICOM_song_14-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-History_created_with_new_CARICOM_song-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       to promote choosing a song that promoted unity and inspired CARICOM identity and pride. A regional panel of judges comprising independent experts in music was nominated by member states and the CARICOM Secretariat. Three rounds of competition condensed 63 entries to a final three, from which judges chose
       <i>
        Celebrating CARICOM
       </i>
       by Michele Henderson of Dominica
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-History_created_with_new_CARICOM_song_14-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-History_created_with_new_CARICOM_song-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       in March 2014.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Song_Factsheet_15-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Song_Factsheet-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Henderson won a US$10,000 prize.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Song_Competition_Terms_of_Reference_16-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Song_Competition_Terms_of_Reference-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Her song was produced by her husband, Roland Delsol Jr., and arranged by Earlson Matthew. It also featured Michael Ferrol on drums and choral input from the St. Alphonsus Choir. It was re-produced for CARICOM by Carl Beaver Henderson of Trinidad and Tobago.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Song_Factsheet_15-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Song_Factsheet-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       A second-place entry titled
       <i>
        My CARICOM
       </i>
       came from Jamaican Adiel Thomas
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-History_created_with_new_CARICOM_song_14-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-History_created_with_new_CARICOM_song-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       who won US$5,000,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Song_Competition_Terms_of_Reference_16-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Song_Competition_Terms_of_Reference-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and a third-place song titled
       <i>
        One CARICOM
       </i>
       by Carmella Lawrence of St. Kitts and Nevis,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-History_created_with_new_CARICOM_song_14-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-History_created_with_new_CARICOM_song-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       won US$2,500.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Song_Competition_Terms_of_Reference_16-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Song_Competition_Terms_of_Reference-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The other songs from the top-ten finalists (in no particular order) were:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <i>
         One Region one Caribbean
        </i>
        from Anguilla,
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         One Caribbean Family
        </i>
        from Jamaica,
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         CARICOM’s Light
        </i>
        from St. Vincent &amp; the Grenadines,
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         We Are CARICOM
        </i>
        from Dominica,
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         Together As one
        </i>
        from Dominica,
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         Blessed CARICOM
        </i>
        from Jamaica,
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         Together We Rise
        </i>
        from Jamaica.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Song_Factsheet_15-2">
         <a href="#cite_note-Song_Factsheet-15">
          [15]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       The first official performance of
       <i>
        Celebrating CARICOM
       </i>
       by Henderson took place on Tuesday 1 July 2014 at the opening ceremony for the Thirty-Fifth Regional Meeting of the Conference of Heads of Government in Antigua and Barbuda.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-History_created_with_new_CARICOM_song_14-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-History_created_with_new_CARICOM_song-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
        History
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Caribbean_Community&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: History">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       CARICOM, originally the Caribbean Community and Common Market, was established by the
       <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Chaguaramas" title="Treaty of Chaguaramas">
        Treaty of Chaguaramas
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
        <a href="#cite_note-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       which took effect on 1 August 1973. The first four signatories were
       <a href="/wiki/Barbados" title="Barbados">
        Barbados
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Jamaica" title="Jamaica">
        Jamaica
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Guyana" title="Guyana">
        Guyana
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Trinidad_and_Tobago" title="Trinidad and Tobago">
        Trinidad and Tobago
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       CARICOM superseded the 1965–1972
       <a href="/wiki/Caribbean_Free_Trade_Association" title="Caribbean Free Trade Association">
        Caribbean Free Trade Association
       </a>
       (CARIFTA) organised to provide a continued economic linkage between the English-speaking countries of the Caribbean after the dissolution of the
       <a href="/wiki/West_Indies_Federation" title="West Indies Federation">
        West Indies Federation
       </a>
       , which lasted from 3 January 1958 to 31 May 1962.
      </p>
      <p>
       A revised Treaty of Chaguaramas established the Caribbean Community including the CARICOM Single Market and Economy (CSME) and was signed by the
       <a href="/wiki/CARICOM_Heads_of_Government" title="CARICOM Heads of Government">
        CARICOM Heads of Government
       </a>
       of the Caribbean Community on 5 July 2001 at their Twenty-Second Meeting of the Conference in Nassau, The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bahamas" title="Bahamas">
        Bahamas
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-revised_chaguaramas_10-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-revised_chaguaramas-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The revised treaty cleared the way to transform the idea of a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Common_market" title="Common market">
        common market
       </a>
       CARICOM into the
       <a href="/wiki/CARICOM_Single_Market_and_Economy" title="CARICOM Single Market and Economy">
        Caribbean (CARICOM) Single Market and Economy
       </a>
       . Part of the revised treaty includes the establishing and implementing the
       <a href="/wiki/Caribbean_Court_of_Justice" title="Caribbean Court of Justice">
        Caribbean Court of Justice
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Haiti's membership in CARICOM remained effectively suspended from 29 February 2004 through early June 2006 following the
       <a href="/wiki/2004_Haitian_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="2004 Haitian coup d'état">
        2004 Haitian coup d'état
       </a>
       and the removal of
       <a href="/wiki/Jean-Bertrand_Aristide" title="Jean-Bertrand Aristide">
        Jean-Bertrand Aristide
       </a>
       from the presidency.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
        <a href="#cite_note-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
        <a href="#cite_note-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       CARICOM announced that no democratically elected government in CARICOM should have its leader deposed. The fourteen other heads of government sought to have Aristide fly from Africa to Jamaica and share his account of events with them, which infuriated the interim Haitian prime minister,
       <a href="/wiki/G%C3%A9rard_Latortue" title="Gérard Latortue">
        Gérard Latortue
       </a>
       , who announced he would take steps to take Haiti out of CARICOM. CARICOM thus voted on suspending the participation of Haitian officials from the councils of CARICOM.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
        <a href="#cite_note-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Following the presidential election of
       <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Pr%C3%A9val" title="René Préval">
        René Préval
       </a>
       , Haitian officials were readmitted and Préval himself gave the opening address at the CARICOM Council of Ministers meeting in July.
      </p>
      <p>
       Since 2013 the CARICOM-bloc and with the Dominican Republic have been tied to the
       <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">
        European Union
       </a>
       via an
       <a href="/wiki/Economic_Partnership_Agreements" title="Economic Partnership Agreements">
        Economic Partnership Agreements
       </a>
       signed in 2008 known as
       <a href="/wiki/CARIFORUM" title="CARIFORUM">
        CARIFORUM
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cpdcngo.org_21-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-cpdcngo.org-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The treaty grants all members of the European Union and CARIFORUM equal rights in terms of trade and investment. Under Article 234 of the agreement, the
       <a href="/wiki/European_Court_of_Justice" title="European Court of Justice">
        European Court of Justice
       </a>
       handles dispute resolution between CARIFORUM and European Union states.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jamaica-gleaner.com_22-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-jamaica-gleaner.com-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>

      <p>
       Thousands of Caricom nationals live within other member states of the Community.
      </p>
      <p>
       An estimated 30,000 Jamaicans legally reside in other CARICOM member states,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30,000_Jamaicans_residing_in_CARICOM_member_states_26-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-30,000_Jamaicans_residing_in_CARICOM_member_states-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       mainly in the Bahamas (5,600),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
        <a href="#cite_note-27">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Antigua &amp; Barbuda (estimated 12,000),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Golding_calls_on_Jamaicans_in_Antigua_28-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Golding_calls_on_Jamaicans_in_Antigua-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Barbados and Trinidad &amp; Tobago).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30,000_Jamaicans_residing_in_CARICOM_member_states_26-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-30,000_Jamaicans_residing_in_CARICOM_member_states-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Also, an estimated 150 Jamaicans live and work in Montserrat.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Golding_calls_on_Jamaicans_in_Antigua_28-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Golding_calls_on_Jamaicans_in_Antigua-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A November 21, 2013 estimated put 16,958 Jamaicans residing illegally in Trinidad &amp; Tobago, as according to the records of the Office of the Chief Immigration Officer, their entry certificates would have since expired.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
        <a href="#cite_note-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       By October 2014, the estimated Jamaicans residing illegally in Trinidad and Tobago was 19,000 along with an estimated 7,169 Barbadians and 25,884 Guyanese residing illegally.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7,000_illegal_Bajans_in_T&amp;T_30-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-7,000_illegal_Bajans_in_T&amp;T-30">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
       An estimated 8,000 Trinidadians and Tobagonians live in Jamaica.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bissessar_celebrates_new_high_commission_31-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Bissessar_celebrates_new_high_commission-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aguas_territoriales_CARICOM.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="800" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Aguas_territoriales_CARICOM.svg/220px-Aguas_territoriales_CARICOM.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Aguas_territoriales_CARICOM.svg/330px-Aguas_territoriales_CARICOM.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Aguas_territoriales_CARICOM.svg/440px-Aguas_territoriales_CARICOM.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aguas_territoriales_CARICOM.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exclusive_Economic_Zones" title="Exclusive Economic Zones">
          Exclusive Economic Zones
         </a>
         of the member states of the CARICOM. Considering them, the total area reaches the 2 300 297 km².
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Barbados hosts a large diaspora population of Guyanese, of whom (in 2005) 5,032 lived there permanently as citizens, permanent residents, immigrants (with immigrant status) and Caricom skilled nationals; 3,200 were residing in Barbados temporarily under work permits, as students, or with "reside and work" status. A further 2,000-3,000 Guyanese were estimated to be living illegally in Barbados at the time.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guyanese,_British_and_Americans_in_Barbados_32-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Guyanese,_British_and_Americans_in_Barbados-32">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Migration between Barbados and Guyana has deep roots, going back over 150 years, with the most intense period of Barbadian migration to then-British Guiana occurring between 1863 and 1886, although as late as the 1920s and 1930s Barbadians were still leaving Barbados for British Guiana.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-When_Barbadians_emigrated_to_Guyana_33-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-When_Barbadians_emigrated_to_Guyana-33">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Migration between Guyana and Suriname also goes back a number of years. An estimated 50,000 Guyanese had migrated to Suriname by 1986
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ethnologue_Suriname_34-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ethnologue_Suriname-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guyanese_Creole_Survey_Report_35-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Guyanese_Creole_Survey_Report-35">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 1987 an estimated 30-40,000 Guyanese were in Suriname.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guyanese_Vital_In_Suriname_36-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Guyanese_Vital_In_Suriname-36">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Many Guyanese left Suriname in the 1970s and 1980s, either voluntarily by expulsion. Over 5,000 were expelled in January 1985 alone.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nervous_neighbours_37-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Nervous_neighbours-37">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
       in the instability Suriname experienced following independence, both coups and civil war.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guyanese_Creole_Survey_Report_35-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Guyanese_Creole_Survey_Report-35">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 2013 an estimated 11,530 Guyanese had emigrated to Suriname and 4,662 Surinamese to Guyana.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guyana_Migration_Profiles_38-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Guyana_Migration_Profiles-38">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Relationship_to_other_supranational_Caribbean_organisations">
        Relationship to other supranational Caribbean organisations
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Caribbean_Community&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Relationship to other supranational Caribbean organisations">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Update" role="presentation">
       <tr>
        <td class="mbox-image">
         <div style="width:52px">
          <img alt="Ambox current red.svg" data-file-height="290" data-file-width="360" height="34" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/42px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/63px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/84px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png 2x" width="42"/>
         </div>
        </td>
        <td class="mbox-text">
         <div class="mbox-text-span">

           updated
          </b>

          </span>

       <div class="thumb tnone">
        <div class="thumbinner" style="width:552px;">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Supranational_Caribbean_Bodies-en.png">
          <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="700" data-file-width="772" height="499" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Supranational_Caribbean_Bodies-en.png/550px-Supranational_Caribbean_Bodies-en.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a0/Supranational_Caribbean_Bodies-en.png 1.5x" width="550"/>
         </a>
         <div class="thumbcaption">
          <div class="magnify">

          Supranational Caribbean Organisations

      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Association_of_Caribbean_States">
        Association of Caribbean States
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Caribbean_Community&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Association of Caribbean States">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       CARICOM was instrumental in the formation of the
       <a href="/wiki/Association_of_Caribbean_States" title="Association of Caribbean States">
        Association of Caribbean States
       </a>
       (ACS) on 24 July 1994. The original idea for the Association came from a recommendation of the West Indian Commission, established in 1989 by the CARICOM heads of state and government. The Commission advocated both deepening the integration process (through the CARICOM Single Market and Economy) and widening it through a separate regional organization encompassing all states in the Caribbean.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Evolution_of_the_ACS_39-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Evolution_of_the_ACS-39">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       CARICOM accepted the commission's recommendations and opened dialogue with other Caribbean states, the Central American states and the Latin American nations of Colombia, Venezuela and Mexico which border the Caribbean, for consultation on the proposals of the West Indian Commission.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Evolution_of_the_ACS_39-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Evolution_of_the_ACS-39">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       At an October 1993 summit the heads of state and government of CARICOM and the presidents of the then-
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/G-3_(Latin_America)" title="G-3 (Latin America)">
        Group of Three
       </a>
       (Colombia, Mexico and Venezuela) formally decided to create an association grouping all states of the Caribbean basin. A work schedule for its formation was adopted. The aim was to create the association in less than a year, an objective which was achieved with the formal creation of the ACS.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Evolution_of_the_ACS_39-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Evolution_of_the_ACS-39">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Community_of_Latin_American_and_Caribbean_States">
        Community of Latin American and Caribbean States
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Caribbean_Community&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Community of Latin American and Caribbean States">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       CARICOM was also involved in the formation of the
       <a href="/wiki/Community_of_Latin_American_and_Caribbean_States" title="Community of Latin American and Caribbean States">
        Community of Latin American and Caribbean States
       </a>
       (CELAC) on 3 December 2010. The idea for CELAC originated at the
       <a href="/wiki/Rio_Group" title="Rio Group">
        Rio Group
       </a>
       –Caribbean Community Unity Summit on 23 February 2010 in Mexico.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
        <a href="#cite_note-40">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
        <a href="#cite_note-41">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
        <a href="#cite_note-42">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mexico's_Latin_American_Summit_43-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Mexico's_Latin_American_Summit-43">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rio_Group_approves_expansion_at_Unity_Summit_44-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Rio_Group_approves_expansion_at_Unity_Summit-44">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="European_Union:_Economic_Partnership_Agreements">
        European Union: Economic Partnership Agreements
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Caribbean_Community&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: European Union: Economic Partnership Agreements">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Since 2013, the CARICOM-bloc and the Dominican Republic have been tied to the
       <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">
        European Union
       </a>
       via an
       <a href="/wiki/Economic_Partnership_Agreements" title="Economic Partnership Agreements">
        Economic Partnership Agreements
       </a>
       known as
       <a href="/wiki/CARIFORUM" title="CARIFORUM">
        CARIFORUM
       </a>
       signed in 2008.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cpdcngo.org_21-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-cpdcngo.org-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The treaty grants all members of the European Union and CARIFORUM equal rights in terms of trade and investment. Within the agreement under Article 234, the
       <a href="/wiki/European_Court_of_Justice" title="European Court of Justice">
        European Court of Justice
       </a>
       also carries dispute resolution mechanisms between CARIFORUM and the European Union states.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jamaica-gleaner.com_22-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-jamaica-gleaner.com-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="OHADAC_Project">
        OHADAC Project
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Caribbean_Community&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: OHADAC Project">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       In May 2016, Caricom's court of original jurisdiction, the CCJ, signed a memorandum of understanding (MOU) with the
       <a href="/wiki/African,_Caribbean_and_Pacific_Group_of_States" title="African, Caribbean and Pacific Group of States">
        ACP
       </a>
       Legal Association based in Guadeloupe recognizing and supporting the goals of implementing a harmonized business law framework in the Caribbean through ACP Legal Association's
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=OHADAC_Project&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="OHADAC Project (page does not exist)">
        OHADAC Project
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CCJ_signs_MOU_on_OHADAC_45-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-CCJ_signs_MOU_on_OHADAC-45">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       OHADAC is the acronym for the French "Organisation pour l'Harmonisation du Droit des Affaires en les Caraïbes", which translates into English as "Organisation for the Harmonization of Business Law in the Caribbean". The OHADAC Project takes inspiration from a
       <a href="/wiki/OHADA" title="OHADA">
        similar organisation in Africa
       </a>
       and aims to enhance economic integration across the entire Caribbean and facilitate increased trade and international investment through unified laws and alternative dispute resolution methods.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CCJ_signs_MOU_on_OHADAC_45-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-CCJ_signs_MOU_on_OHADAC-45">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="References">
        References
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">

        <li id="cite_note-7">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-7">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          <a class="external text" href="http://www.caricom.org/jsp/pressreleases/press_releases_2012/pres185_12.jsp" rel="nofollow">
           "Communiqué Issued at the Conclusion of the Thirty-Third Regular Meeting of the Conference of Heads of Government of the Caribbean Community, 4-6 July 2012, Gros Islet, Saint Lucia"
          </a>
          , "Heads of Government recognized that, although English was the official language of the Community, the facility to communicate in their languages could enhance the participation of Haiti and Suriname in the integration process. They therefore requested the conduct of a study to examine the possibilities and implications, including costs, of introducing French and Dutch."
         </span>
